  //<![CDATA[
    function loadScript(url, callback) {
      var script = document.createElement("script");
      script.type = "text/javascript";
    
      if (script.readyState) {  //Internet Explorer
          script.onreadystatechange = function() {
            if (script.readyState == "loaded" ||
                    script.readyState == "complete") {
              script.onreadystatechange = null;
              callback();
            }
          };
      } else {  //Other browsers
        script.onload = function() {
          callback();
        };
      }
    
      script.src = url;
      document.getElementsByTagName("head")[0].appendChild(script);
    }
    
    function initAppleId() {
      AppleID.auth.init({
        clientId : 'com.goodreads.app', 
        scope : 'name email',
        redirectURI: 'https://www.goodreads.com/apple_users/sign_in_with_apple_web',
        state: 'apple_oauth_state_949f0976-fee9-415d-931a-04b72b8560c8'
      });
    }

<div id="descriptionContainer">
  
      <div id="description" class="readable stacked" style="right:0">
        
<span id="freeTextContainer13102415029920862451">The originality, concentrated power and ‘fierce indignation’ of his satirical writing have earned Jonathan Swift a reputation as the greatest prose satirist in English literature. <i>Gulliver’s Travels</i> is, of course, his world renowned masterpiece in the genre; however, Swift wrote other, shorter works that also offer excellent evidence of his inspired lampoonery. Perhaps the</span>
  <span id="freeText13102415029920862451" style="display:none">The originality, concentrated power and ‘fierce indignation’ of his satirical writing have earned Jonathan Swift a reputation as the greatest prose satirist in English literature. <i>Gulliver’s Travels</i> is, of course, his world renowned masterpiece in the genre; however, Swift wrote other, shorter works that also offer excellent evidence of his inspired lampoonery. Perhaps the most famous of these is <i>A Modest Proposal</i>, in which he straight-facedly suggests that Ireland could solve its hunger problems by using its children for food. Also included in this collection are <i>The Battle of Books, A Meditation upon a Broomstick, A Discourse Concerning the Mechanical Operations of the Spirit</i> and <i>An Argument Against Abolishing Christianity in England</i>.<br />This inexpensive edition will certainly be welcomed by teachers and students of English literature, but its appeal extends to any reader who delights in watching a master satirist wield words as weapons.</span>
  <a data-text-id="13102415029920862451" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer135425" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer3576107071087724946">I originally had two shelves: <br/><br/>books that make me want to have children so I can read to them<br/><br/>and<br/><br/>books that make me want to have children so I can eat them<br/><br/>But really this is the only one that would fall in the latter category.<br/><br/>This is one of the greatest pieces of satire ever written, but seriously, have you ever noticed that babies really do taste better? Think about it. Veal, lamb, kittens. I could go on.</span>

          </span>

<div id="review_1902426842" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1902426842" />
    <a title="Paul E. Morph" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/44824883-paul-e-morph"><img alt="Paul E. Morph" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1602970987p2/44824883.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1902426842?book_show_action=true">Apr 21, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Paul E. Morph" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Paul E. Morph" href="/user/show/44824883-paul-e-morph">Paul E. Morph</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Kindle Edition " href="/book/show/8557951-a-modest-proposal-and-other-satirical-works">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/44824883-paul-e-morph?shelf=books-read-in-2017">books-read-in-2017</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1902426842" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer9403440342488544064">This collection of Jonathan Swift's satirical works is very witty, very clever and very well-written. You do need a reasonably good knowledge of the times in which he wrote to appreciate much of it but I enjoyed it a great deal and I'm very far from being a historian.<br/><br/>When this is funny, which is a lot, it is very funny indeed. It's also thought-provoking; particularly when he addresses issues which still haven't been resolved to this day. I can't quite bring myself to give it the full five stars</span>
  <span id="freeText9403440342488544064" style="display:none">This collection of Jonathan Swift's satirical works is very witty, very clever and very well-written. You do need a reasonably good knowledge of the times in which he wrote to appreciate much of it but I enjoyed it a great deal and I'm very far from being a historian.<br /><br />When this is funny, which is a lot, it is very funny indeed. It's also thought-provoking; particularly when he addresses issues which still haven't been resolved to this day. I can't quite bring myself to give it the full five stars, however, because there are passages contained herein which are quite tedious and a bit of a slog to get through. They're very much the exception rather than the rule, though, and this is a great read overall.<br /><br />I can only imagine what an outcry 'A Modest Proposal' must have caused when originally published. The people I've spoken to about it who didn't know the basic premise have found it quite shocking even today.</span>
  <a data-text-id="9403440342488544064" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

              <span id="reviewTextContainer1328278730" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer6522897925502988392">This is obviously an incredible satire, which hopes to give some satisfaction to the rich. I recently reread it after reading <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/22874450.The_Sorrows_of_Young_Mike' title='The Sorrows of Young Mike by John Zelazny' rel='nofollow noopener'>The Sorrows of Young Mike</a>. In <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/8430796.John_Zelazny' title='John Zelazny' rel='nofollow noopener'>John Zelazny</a>'s parody, the main character parodies <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/1831.Jonathan_Swift' title='Jonathan Swift' rel='nofollow noopener'>Jonathan Swift</a>'s modest proposal. It is a parody within a parody and the modern twist is displayed well. </span>
  <span id="freeText6522897925502988392" style="display:none">This is obviously an incredible satire, which hopes to give some satisfaction to the rich. I recently reread it after reading <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/22874450.The_Sorrows_of_Young_Mike" title="The Sorrows of Young Mike by John Zelazny" rel="nofollow noopener">The Sorrows of Young Mike</a>. In <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/8430796.John_Zelazny" title="John Zelazny" rel="nofollow noopener">John Zelazny</a>'s parody, the main character parodies <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/1831.Jonathan_Swift" title="Jonathan Swift" rel="nofollow noopener">Jonathan Swift</a>'s modest proposal. It is a parody within a parody and the modern twist is displayed well. </span>

  <div class="friendReviews elementListBrown" >
    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_1249839371" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1249839371" />
    <a title="sologdin" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/5610133-sologdin"><img alt="sologdin" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1307928658p2/5610133.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1249839371?book_show_action=true">Apr 08, 2015</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="sologdin" class="user" itemprop="url" name="sologdin" href="/user/show/5610133-sologdin">sologdin</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780879759193" href="/book/show/2679.A_Modest_Proposal_and_Other_Satirical_Works">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5610133-sologdin?shelf=of-best-sentence-and-moost-solaas">of-best-sentence-and-moost-solaas</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1249839371" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer16798664778680905133">Nutshell: misanthropic rightwinger thinks that he’s funny, but he’s just a dick. <br/> <br/>The foregoing conclusions are authorized by the author, who admitted in a letter to fellow douchebag Pope: <blockquote>I have got materials toward a treatise proving the falsity of that definition <i>animal rationale</i>, and to show that it would be only <i>rationis capax</i>. Upon this great foundation of misanthropy, … the whole building of my Travels is erected. (21)</blockquote>Several texts in this collection:<br/><br/><i>A Tale of a Tub</i>--<br/>Lots of derridean out</span>
  <span id="freeText16798664778680905133" style="display:none">Nutshell: misanthropic rightwinger thinks that he’s funny, but he’s just a dick. <br /> <br />The foregoing conclusions are authorized by the author, who admitted in a letter to fellow douchebag Pope: <blockquote>I have got materials toward a treatise proving the falsity of that definition <i>animal rationale</i>, and to show that it would be only <i>rationis capax</i>. Upon this great foundation of misanthropy, … the whole building of my Travels is erected. (21)</blockquote>Several texts in this collection:<br /><br /><i>A Tale of a Tub</i>--<br />Lots of derridean outworks here, including the opening dedication to some inbred aristocrat <blockquote>I should now, in right of a dedicator, give your Lordship a list of your own virtues, and at the same time be very unwilling to offend your modesty; but chiefly I should celebrate your liberality towards men of great parts and small fortunes, and give you broad hints that I mean myself. And I was just going on in the usual method to peruse a hundred or two of dedications, and transcribe an abstract to be applied to your Lordship. (27)</blockquote>Fourth such outwork explains the title: <blockquote>seamen have a custom when they meet a Whale to fling him out an empty Tub, by way of amusement, to divert him from laying violent hands upon the Ship. This parable was immediately mythologised; the Whale was interpreted to be Hobbes’ “Leviathan,” which tosses and plays with all other schemes of religion and government, whereof a great many are hollow, and dry, and empty, and noisy, and wooden, and given to rotation. This is the Leviathan from whence the terrible wits of our age are said to borrow their weapons. The Ship in danger is easily understood to be its old antitype the commonwealth. (39-40)</blockquote>Preface otherwise makes sure to avoid going forward “without declaiming, according to custom, against the multitude of writers whereof the whole multitude of writers most reasonably complain” (40). Speaker of the preface notes that in England it’s fine to state openly that “we live in the very dregs of time” (46)—not sure how to take that, as the layers of irony here are numerous—but it would be consistent with the retrograde politics.<br /><br />The ‘Tale’ proper proceeds as an allegory of three guys (Catholic, Lutheran, Calvinist) who inherit cloaks (church praxis) from their father’s will (scripture) and go about dicking up their cloaks. This allegory is intermingled with digressions. The Introduction makes plain that the text is concerned with “oratorical machines” (50), from whose company attorneys are apparently excluded (?). We see that the scheme of “oratorical receptacles or machines contains a great mystery, being a type, a sign, an emblem, a shadow, a symbol, bearing analogy to the spacious commonwealth of writers and to those methods by which they must exalt themselves to a certain eminency above the inferior world” (53). <br /><br />Most interesting is the use of ellipsis to omit materials (as we may have noted regarding <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/1913044.Gulliver_s_Travels__Classics_S_" title="Gulliver's Travels (Classics S) by Jonathan Swift" rel="nofollow noopener"> Gulliver’s Travels</a>). Author has a ‘hiatus’ regarding “faction” (54) and regarding Calvinism (140). (In the “Mechanical Operation” text, infra, he also specifically omits the explanation of “the whole scheme of spiritual mechanism,” i.e., ostensibly the point of the text, because “it was thought neither safe nor convenient to print it” (162).) Best elision, from the famous essay on madness, while working through the important problem of “how it is possible to account for such imaginations as these in particular men, without recourse to my phenomenon of vapours ascending from the lower faculties to overshadow the brain, and there distilling into conceptions” (118-19) (which is substantially identical to Ayn Rand’s epistemology, no?): “There is in mankind a certain […] and this I take to be a clear solution of the matter [!]” (120). So, there it is.<br /><br />Nice jab at ‘critics,’ insofar as we are told: <blockquote>For it hath been objected that those ancient heroes, famous for their combating so many giants and dragons and robbers, were in their own persons a greater nuisance to mankind than any of the monsters they subdued; and therefore, to render their obligations more complete, when all other vermin were destroyed, should in conscience have concluded with the same justice upon themselves, as Hercules most generously did. (72)</blockquote>Criticism is thereafter cunningly identified with the intention<blockquote>to travel through this vast world of writings; to peruse and hunt those monstrous faults bred within them; to drag out the lurking errors, like Cacus from his den; to multiply them like Hydra’s heads; and rake them together like Augeas’ dung; or else drive away a sort of dangerous fowl who have a perverse inclination to plunder the best branches of the tree of knowledge, like those Stymphalian birds that ate up the fruit. (73)</blockquote> So, good to see that he has developed an enlightened attitude toward his interlocutors, for whom, I think, he has just recommended suicide.<br /><br />On the other hand, text will, at another moment, with perhaps a different speaker, suggest that Homer, “a person not without some abilities, and for ancient of a tolerable genius,” is nevertheless full of “many gross errors” (92).<br /><br />Anyway, have dwelled overlong on the “Tale,” which is first rate overall. Much of interest that I haven’t mentioned. Suffice to say that one speaker recommends a derridean oblique approach: <blockquote>get a thorough insight into the index by which the whole book is governed and turned, like fishes by the tail. For to enter the palace of learning at the great gate requires an expense of time and forms, therefore men of much haste and little ceremony are content to get in by the backdoor. For the arts are all in a flying march, and therefore more easily subdued by attacking from the rear. (104) </blockquote>“A Discourse Concerning the Mechanical Operation of the Spirit”—<br />Opens with the suggestion that Mohammed “is known to have borrowed a moiety of his religious system from the Christian faith”(153), and then descends to the bizarre proposition that “there are three general ways of ejaculating the soul” (155). Inter alia, deduces a “history of fanaticism” (167 ff.). <br /><br />After the ‘Tale,’ we have “A Tritical Essay,” “Meditation Upon a Broomstick,” “On Political Lying,” “The Drapier Letters,” and “A Character, Panegyric, and Description of the Legion Club,” about which little need be said. Also included:<br /><br />“Thoughts on Various Subjects”—<br />A collection of generally non-satirical gnomics. I fucking hate the gnomic. Here, author outs himself as troglodyte with such items as “Law in a free country is, or ought to be, the determination of the majority of those who have property in land” (193). Uh, <i>fuck you</i>? Also: “Those who are against religion must needs be fools” (195). Whatever? This text also includes the famous ‘confederacy of dunces’ line deployed later by Toole.<br /><br />“An Argument Against Abolishing Christianity in England”—<br />Builds a distinction between “nominal” and “real” Christianity; he won’t defend the latter, as it has “been for some time wholly laid aside by general consent as utterly inconsistent with our present schemes of wealth and power” (201), which strikes me as a nasty disqualificatory thesis. Seriously, you have a state church and you wrote this to oppose repealing the Test Act, no? (“Nothing can be more notorious than that the Atheists, Deists, Socinians, Antitrinitarians, and other subdivisions of freethinkers, are persons of little zeal for the present ecclesiastical establishment; their declared opinion is for repealing the sacramental test” (210). FFS. <i>FFS!</i>)<br /><br /> “A Modest Proposal”—<br />Obviously one of the great essays in English literary history. One thing I note now in reading through this time is that the impetus for the eponymous proposal is that “I have been assured by a very knowing American of my acquaintance in London, that a young healthy child well nursed is at a year old a most delicious, nourishing, and wholesome food, whether stewed, roasted, baked, or boiled; and I make no doubt that it will equally serve in a fricassee or a ragout,” which is the first overt reference to cannibalism in the text (259). <br /><br />(No “Battle of the Books,” weirdly.)<br /><br />Recommended for those who think praise was originally a pension paid by the world, readers affected in the head by tentiginous humour, and persons who have no children by which they can propose to get a single penny.<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="16798664778680905133" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer14558522536514624357">Swift's satire, A Modest Proposal, was not well-known or well-read in his life.   <span id="freeText14558522536514624357" style="display:none">Swift's satire, A Modest Proposal, was not well-known or well-read in his life. Of course, given the nature of the piece--the desperate need for change in Ireland--lack of recognition was difficult.<br /><br />I have read and taught this many times. Most students don't understand the depths of the satire or the excellent argument structure presented in this essay.<br /><br />Swift's ability to develop his argument in the way he has makes the piece an excellent read for anyone looking to understand the many forms of developing argument.</span>

<div id="review_543544217" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/543544217" />
    <a title="J." class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/17241396-j"><img alt="J." src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1361642264p2/17241396.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/543544217?book_show_action=true">Feb 22, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="J." class="user" itemprop="url" name="J." href="/user/show/17241396-j">J.</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/17241396-j?shelf=irish">irish</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/17241396-j?shelf=classics">classics</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/17241396-j?shelf=fiction">fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/17241396-j?shelf=philosophy">philosophy</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/17241396-j?shelf=humour">humour</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer543544217" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer18240158824483339990">'A Modest Proposal for Preventing the Children of Poor People From Being a Burthen to Their Parents or Country, and for Making Them Beneficial to the Publick' was published anonymously in 1729. These were bad years in Ireland three failed harvests were followed by poverty and disease. 'A modest proposal... ' lays bare the politics and prejudice of the time. The structure of the pamphlet imitates the pamphlets being published which offered up serious proposals to the crisis.<br/><br/>The shocking suggestio</span>
  <span id="freeText18240158824483339990" style="display:none">'A Modest Proposal for Preventing the Children of Poor People From Being a Burthen to Their Parents or Country, and for Making Them Beneficial to the Publick' was published anonymously in 1729. These were bad years in Ireland three failed harvests were followed by poverty and disease. 'A modest proposal... ' lays bare the politics and prejudice of the time. The structure of the pamphlet imitates the pamphlets being published which offered up serious proposals to the crisis.<br /><br />The shocking suggestion is that the poor Irish should sell their children to the rich like cattle in order to gain financially. "A young healthy child well nursed, is, at a year old, a most delicious nourishing and wholesome food, whether stewed, roasted, baked, or boiled; and I make no doubt that it will equally serve in a fricassee, or a ragout." He discusses the benefits of such commerce to butchers, for the making of gloves for ladies and boots for men. He also suggests that there will be no more domestic violence as women will be valued for child bearing. There were rumours that people indulged in cannibalism but those rumours existed in Edmund Spensers day.<br /><br />At the time William Petty was surveyor in Ireland and worked for Cromwell, he mapped and measured creditors, what was taken and given to soldiers and the cronies of Cromwell. These statistics were part of a belief in a mathematical solution. The Irish economy was extractive and produce was taken and sold in England.<br /><br />Swift was Dean of St. Patrick's cathedral a position partly of exile and due to failure in his career. In this work he is emulating his sermons, satirising people who think they can reform and holding forth that people will ever change. He contributed to public arguments about how Ireland was ruled. In the 1720's he objected anonymously via the 'Drapiers letters' to the underhanded winning of a contract to recoin the currency without the consultation of the Anglo Irish community. He became a hero for this intervention. Although Swift denunciates he doesn't necessarily sympathise with the people. I enjoyed this and I encourage visitors to Dublin to visit St Patrick's cathedral.<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="18240158824483339990" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_9962526" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/9962526" />
    <a title="Nicky" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/210867-nicky"><img alt="Nicky" src="https://s.gr-assets.com/assets/nophoto/user/m_50x66-82093808bca726cb3249a493fbd3bd0f.png" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/9962526?book_show_action=true">Dec 04, 2007</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Nicky" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Nicky" href="/user/show/210867-nicky">Nicky</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>


          <div><span class="uitext greyText">Recommends it for: </span>
           <span class="uitext reviewText">thinkers, people who appreciate humor</span></div>

        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/210867-nicky?shelf=philosophy">philosophy</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer9962526" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10325809993094861278"><b>
  <i>A Modest Proposal and Other Satirical Works</i>
</b> contains five essays by Swift. <b>
  <i>A Modest Proposal</i>
</b> focuses upon politics, <b>
  <i>Battle of the Books</i>
</b> upon literature and philosophy, and the remaining three address religious belief and practice.<br/><br/><b>
  <i>A Modest Proposal</i>
</b> is easily the most famous of Swift's essays, and as such most people are aware of its premise. It is incredibly witty, brief, and poignant. A fine satire.<br/><br/>To appreciate <b>
  <i>Battle of the Books</i>
</b> requires a fair amount of understanding rega</span>
  <span id="freeText10325809993094861278" style="display:none"><b>
  <i>A Modest Proposal and Other Satirical Works</i>
</b> contains five essays by Swift. <b>
  <i>A Modest Proposal</i>
</b> focuses upon politics, <b>
  <i>Battle of the Books</i>
</b> upon literature and philosophy, and the remaining three address religious belief and practice.<br /><br /><b>
  <i>A Modest Proposal</i>
</b> is easily the most famous of Swift's essays, and as such most people are aware of its premise. It is incredibly witty, brief, and poignant. A fine satire.<br /><br />To appreciate <b>
  <i>Battle of the Books</i>
</b> requires a fair amount of understanding regarding ancient and modern philosophy and literature. It casts writings (personified as their writers) in struggle against one another set in a library. But in a broader sense, it can be appreciated as expressing vanity, ingratitude, and disdain by some modern thinkers for ancient thinkers.<br /><br /><b>
  <i>Meditation Upon a Broomstick</i>
</b> is a very short work drawing analogy between broomstick and man, speaking of the nature and purposes of each.<br /><br /><b>
  <i>An Argument Against Abolishing Christianity in England</i>
</b><br />is a satire against the idea that shortcomings in human nature are religious faults. Even if religion were removed, vices due to human nature would still trouble mankind.<br /><br /><b>
  <i>Discourse Concerning the Mechanical Operation of the Spirit</i>
</b> is a satire directed against all religious extremists. Swift refers to Muslims and Protestant Christians of his own age in particular. I can't help but think how relevant Swift remains more than 250 years after his death.</span>
  <a data-text-id="10325809993094861278" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_112914832" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/112914832" />
    <a title="Mark Bratkowski" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/2144659-mark-bratkowski"><img alt="Mark Bratkowski" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1483586121p2/2144659.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/112914832?book_show_action=true">Jul 22, 2010</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Mark Bratkowski" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Mark Bratkowski" href="/user/show/2144659-mark-bratkowski">Mark Bratkowski</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback " href="/book/show/8013643-a-modest-proposal-and-other-satirical-works">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2144659-mark-bratkowski?shelf=world-literature">world-literature</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2144659-mark-bratkowski?shelf=nonfiction">nonfiction</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer112914832" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer3294875513731211346">This is another book that I read to teach at Ursuline next year. Jonathan Swift's "A Modest Proposal" is without a doubt one of the most intellectual and humorous pieces of satire ever written. Another satiric essay that I liked was "An Argument Against Abolishing Christianity in England". This was written earlier than "A Modest Proposal" but uses soome of the same devices. Swift's evidence shows how ending Christianity would bring political and economic benefits to England. Of course, his argum</span>
  <span id="freeText3294875513731211346" style="display:none">This is another book that I read to teach at Ursuline next year. Jonathan Swift's "A Modest Proposal" is without a doubt one of the most intellectual and humorous pieces of satire ever written. Another satiric essay that I liked was "An Argument Against Abolishing Christianity in England". This was written earlier than "A Modest Proposal" but uses soome of the same devices. Swift's evidence shows how ending Christianity would bring political and economic benefits to England. Of course, his argument is sarcastic and in opposition to his own beliefs; Swift was an ordained Anglican minister. <br /><br />I wasn't nearly as impressed with the other satirical writings. "The Battle of the Books" is about a battle between books written by ancient and modern writers. I felt left out while I was reading it because I didn't know who the "modern" writers of the 17th and 18th centuries were. The work also wasn't finished and that left me at a loss.<br /><br />I would like to read Swift's magnum opus, "Guliver's Travels", some day. However, I was disappointed by the overall body of his work in this set of essays. I found him to be highly witty, but he could also get too philosophical and meandering, and I often lost my interest in his writing. </span>
  <a data-text-id="3294875513731211346" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_2753285146" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2753285146" />
    <a title="Chris" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/5129776-chris"><img alt="Chris" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1331301021p2/5129776.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2753285146?book_show_action=true">Mar 17, 2019</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Chris" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Chris" href="/user/show/5129776-chris">Chris</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5129776-chris?shelf=satire">satire</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2753285146" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer7566966451844644232"><blockquote>I have been assured by a very knowing American of my acquaintance in London, that a young healthy child well nursed is at a year old a most delicious, nourishing, and wholesome food, whether stewed, roasted, baked, or boiled; and I make no doubt that it will equally serve in a fricassee or a ragout.</blockquote><br/>With this paragraph, around a quarter of the way through a 1729 text, Swift (originally writing anonymously) detonates the bomb that is at the core of <i>A Modest PROPOSAL For preventing the CHILDREN of </i></span>
  <span id="freeText7566966451844644232" style="display:none"><blockquote>I have been assured by a very knowing American of my acquaintance in London, that a young healthy child well nursed is at a year old a most delicious, nourishing, and wholesome food, whether stewed, roasted, baked, or boiled; and I make no doubt that it will equally serve in a fricassee or a ragout.</blockquote><br />With this paragraph, around a quarter of the way through a 1729 text, Swift (originally writing anonymously) detonates the bomb that is at the core of <i>A Modest PROPOSAL For preventing the CHILDREN of POOR PEOPLE From being a Burthen to Their Parents or Country, and For making them Beneficial to the PUBLICK</i>.<br /><br />But this, of course, is Swift, and we must never take his writings at their word. When he discusses the main advantages of such a policy for Ireland (such as fewer Catholics, the introduction of a new dish for gentlemen with refined tastes, an added draw for taverns, an income for the 'breeders' and an economic policy to encourage marriage) his purpose is to criticise social attitudes, but as with all satire, outward appearances are outrageous--but also deceptive.<br /><br />Swift was Anglo-Irish Anglican clergyman, and his position was to be a signpost always to a <em>via media</em> (as characterises the Church of England itself, being somewhere in the middle of a Christian continuum stretching from Dissenter to Roman Catholic). By taking arguments to extremes, as with <em>A Modest Proposal</em>, he exposed what he saw as inherent ridiculousness, but with such po-faced earnestness that it was sometimes hard to know when he was being serious without close reading of the text.<br /><br />In this slim volume are also included four other works. <em>The Battle of the Books </em>is the longest, and was essentially a discourse on the three strands of Christianity in the west, with the individuals Peter, Martin and Jack standing for Catholicism, Anglicanism and Nonconformism. (As a digression, I wonder if this piece indirectly influenced R M Ballantyne's famous novel <em>The Coral Island</em>, the leads of which were Peterkin, Jack Martin and Ralph, and which itself directly inspired William Golding's characters Piggy, Jack and Ralph in <em>The Lord of the Flies</em>.)<br /><br />Also here is the very short <em>A Meditation upon a Broomstick</em>, a mock allegory of the human condition perpetrated as a joke upon a Lady Berkeley. This is followed by <em>A Discourse concerning the Mechanical Operation of the Spirit</em>: in this Swift equates spirit with 'enthusiasm', literally the state of being possessed by a god. The manifestation of enthusiasm Swift calls 'ejaculating the spirit, or transporting it beyond the sphere of matter'; to the three expressions of this manifestation--divine prophecy or inspiration, devilish possession, and the product of the imagination or strong emotions--Swift adds 'the mechanical operation of the spirit', which he at first compares to the ass on which Mohammed is said to have travelled to Paradise. (He also has witty words to say about epistolatory conventions, but there is no space, dear reader, to expand on this.)<br /><br />That only leaves the last of these papers published before 1729, <em>An Argument Against Abolishing Christianity in England</em>, which, however dry the subject appears to be from the title, is as knockabout a farce attacking all and sundry as any in this collection. Swift's own footnotes, along with the editor's, are included here, as well as a brief biography by way of introduction.<br /><br />Even allowing for a three-century gap these pieces have a surprisingly relevant contemporary bite, especially in view of recent political events: the shocking satire of <em>A Modest Proposal</em> throws a light on the downsides of utilitarianism, the dangers of cynical commercialism and the human capacity for self-delusion.</span>
  <a data-text-id="7566966451844644232" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_17054808" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/17054808" />
    <a title="Matt" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/54697-matt"><img alt="Matt" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1353271036p2/54697.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/17054808?book_show_action=true">Mar 05, 2008</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Matt" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Matt" href="/user/show/54697-matt">Matt</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/54697-matt?shelf=satire">satire</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/54697-matt?shelf=social-crit">social-crit</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/54697-matt?shelf=fictions-of-the-big-it">fictions-of-the-big-it</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer17054808" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer15805709493155732107"><br/><br/>"A Modest Proposal" is so fucking ridiculously contemporary that I can't help but be the one to say it for the millionth time. If you think things have gotten too raw and uncivilized in today's age, and that people were more well mannered in the olden days, you are....full O' SHITE!<br/><br/>I'm sure no one reading this actually does think this way but still...I love being able to add this bit of actual, factual info and not feeling the least bit bad about it because history is genteel only to the people </span>
  <span id="freeText15805709493155732107" style="display:none"><br /><br />"A Modest Proposal" is so fucking ridiculously contemporary that I can't help but be the one to say it for the millionth time. If you think things have gotten too raw and uncivilized in today's age, and that people were more well mannered in the olden days, you are....full O' SHITE!<br /><br />I'm sure no one reading this actually does think this way but still...I love being able to add this bit of actual, factual info and not feeling the least bit bad about it because history is genteel only to the people who see the past through rose-colored glasses which are dipped in (bull) shit...<br /><br />I also like that Swift was a great hater of everything...also, unless I'm mistaken, a royalist conservative and a pretty trenchant religious one at that....and yet, and yet, as Yeats was wont to say: "world besotted traveler he/ served the cause of human liberty"<br /><br />So SNL, the Daily Show, Carlin, Bill Hicks, Lenny Bruce et al were 200 years in the making (and that's just talkin' Swift here, mind you)...<br /><br />Also I remember reading that prominent literary critic of his once remarked that the narrator this book has what he brilliantly refers to as "a nervous hiccup"- he's not being outwardly snotty, there's no punk rock-ishness intended. You can hear it if you listen, its in the last sentence. The narrator's more like an acquiescent bureaucrat sincerely attempting to remedy the situation at hand for the colonial overlords...who happen to be enslaving his own people with exorbitant rents and property rights which leave much to be desired....which is pretty much where I think a satirist is these days. <br /><br />If you look at the characters or personae your average comedian or satirical talk show host (ahem, Stewart and Colbert, especially, which gives it a different spin entirely) has, its all about being a sort of befuddled, confused, average guy who is trying to make these lumbering, incoherent systems and bizarre situations run smoothly. The technocrats are mad. The comedian is a member of the lower order, too smart and too normal to be from the avant- outside, anxiously clearing his throat and calling the masters of the universe into question using irony, paradox, scatology, and an almost childlike sense of absurdity to show how wrong and possibly evil the powers that be are, how they show themselves to be almost without fail. <br /><br />It's not that they stand outside the system- it's that they are a part of the madness and therefore have a front row seat to the insanity in high places. Butchering of language (that precious gift), the unnecessary deaths of innocents, baldfaced lies, cynicism to assume that such things are or should be the due matter of course, sinister opportunities resulting in collateral damage and mind-boggling failure and outrageous profits for shady people who everyone already pretty much knows to be shady, deep down, because they are a part of it too. <br />\<br /><br />So, therefore, all the proposals are modest. Immodesty wouldn't notice itself without the contrast. Swift lives! <br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="15805709493155732107" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer15118622860236591668">The reason for picking up <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/2677.A_Modest_Proposal_and_Other_Satirical_Works' title='A Modest Proposal and Other Satirical Works by Jonathan Swift' rel='nofollow noopener'>A Modest Proposal and Other Satirical Works</a> was that I wanted to reread <i>A Modest Proposal</i>, which I had previously read in my copy of <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/13501074.The_Norton_Anthology_of_English_Literature_Vol_1' title='The Norton Anthology of English Literature Vol 1 by M.H. Abrams' rel='nofollow noopener'>The Norton Anthology of English Literature: Vol 1</a> and which I still think is a masterpiece and as such probably the best satire I've ever come across.<br/><br/>As a sort of ‘bonus’ I had four more of Swift’s satirical writings in this book, each of which I liked.   <span id="freeText15118622860236591668" style="display:none">The reason for picking up <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/2677.A_Modest_Proposal_and_Other_Satirical_Works" title="A Modest Proposal and Other Satirical Works by Jonathan Swift" rel="nofollow noopener">A Modest Proposal and Other Satirical Works</a> was that I wanted to reread <i>A Modest Proposal</i>, which I had previously read in my copy of <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/13501074.The_Norton_Anthology_of_English_Literature_Vol_1" title="The Norton Anthology of English Literature Vol 1 by M.H. Abrams" rel="nofollow noopener">The Norton Anthology of English Literature: Vol 1</a> and which I still think is a masterpiece and as such probably the best satire I've ever come across.<br /><br />As a sort of ‘bonus’ I had four more of Swift’s satirical writings in this book, each of which I liked. I must add though, that by far the most of his satirical writings require (a lot of) background knowledge into the period they were written in. Should you possess this background knowledge, then please, read this little book, as Swift’s satirical writings are fabulous!<br /><br />See also the Netherlands &amp; Flanders group, message 100 of their <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/topic/show/1873328-zomer-2014?order=a&amp;page=2" rel="nofollow noopener">Summer Challenge 2014</a>, for a review in Dutch.</span>

<tr class="no_border feedFooterReview" id="update_comment_stuff_Review1002559634">

  <span id="freeText12984293088141890634" style="display:none">This is one of my favorite satirical works of all time in which Swift proposes, to solve the problem of the poverty and starvation rampant in Ireland, that the poor Irish eat their own children to stave off hunger. <br /><br />"I have been assured by a very knowing American of my acquaintance in London, that a young healthy child well nursed, is, at a year old, a most delicious nourishing and wholesome food, whether stewed, roasted, baked, or boiled; and I make no doubt that it will equally serve in a fricasie, or a ragoust."<br /><br />Propositions of cannibalism make me giggle. :)</span>

<span id="freeTextContainer10721606505634408125">Buying a 412 page book for what turns out to be the nine page Proposal, is a waste of money. How fascinated would you be to read excerpts of a private journal recording thoughts on the mundane and on persons you have no knowledge of, nor interest in? Would you rather read copies of private letters? Much of the book displays Swift’s caustic wit, but, unless you are an enthusiast for Irish history, there is so much minutia that a quarter of the volume is given to footnotes! Save your money and fin</span>
  <span id="freeText10721606505634408125" style="display:none">Buying a 412 page book for what turns out to be the nine page Proposal, is a waste of money. How fascinated would you be to read excerpts of a private journal recording thoughts on the mundane and on persons you have no knowledge of, nor interest in? Would you rather read copies of private letters? Much of the book displays Swift’s caustic wit, but, unless you are an enthusiast for Irish history, there is so much minutia that a quarter of the volume is given to footnotes! Save your money and find “A Modest Proposal” in another edition.</span>

<span id="freeTextContainer6377705003998956947">A Modest Proposal was great. So were the rules for servants. Jonathan Swift is a master satirist. So, 4 stars to the funny papers, and 2 stars to the boring letters, with an average of 3 stars.</span>

          </span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3595415996" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer3405854056782022212">This is a small collection of essays and letters. It's quite a subjective read as each piece demonstrates Swift's range as a writer of the political, satirical, theological and personal. I would say it is a taster for hunting down further collections depending on what you are interested in. For me, I enjoyed A Modest Proposal, A Meditation Upon a Broomstick, and Thoughts on Various Subjects. The other pieces were interesting but at times rambling. Don't get me wrong, I like rambling writing but </span>
  <span id="freeText3405854056782022212" style="display:none">This is a small collection of essays and letters. It's quite a subjective read as each piece demonstrates Swift's range as a writer of the political, satirical, theological and personal. I would say it is a taster for hunting down further collections depending on what you are interested in. For me, I enjoyed A Modest Proposal, A Meditation Upon a Broomstick, and Thoughts on Various Subjects. The other pieces were interesting but at times rambling. Don't get me wrong, I like rambling writing but find it strenuous when it pertains to political or religious content. He's not as acerbic as I like when it comes to satire, and I personally prefer Chesterton and Shaw in that respect but his writing is enjoyable, not so much when it comes to a turn of phrase but in summary of a well turned out opinion. </span>

<span id="freeTextContainer14108766391489041712">First off, A Modest Proposal is fantastic. I imagine the humor to be ages ahead of it's time, completely invested in this false voice and hilarious. I'm not sure I've ever fallen asleep more then when trying to read Battle of the Books, and Mechanical Operation of the Spirit. Once I finished with them, I was like hey not so bad.   <span id="freeText14108766391489041712" style="display:none">First off, A Modest Proposal is fantastic. I imagine the humor to be ages ahead of it's time, completely invested in this false voice and hilarious. It is five stars plus.<br /><br />The other stories though, man, they are hard sledding. I'm not sure I've ever fallen asleep more then when trying to read Battle of the Books, and Mechanical Operation of the Spirit. Once I finished with them, I was like hey not so bad. But getting through that first read was hard work. <br /><br />Read A Modest Proposal and enjoy it, but for the rest, I'd say take it on only if you are fond of your dictionary and long trains of thought that require focus and concentration to follow.</span>

  function submitShelfLink(unique_id, book_id, shelf_id, shelf_name, submit_form, exclusive) {
    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked && exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked
      new Ajax.Request('/shelf/add_to_shelf', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name)}, parameters:'book_id=' + book_id + '&name=' + shelf_name + '&a=' + action + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('CUILr4OfAMAp8LrR6P9bfFV1gGC6OLg2AM7B3UfiOFYzZGJ9OMHJqI7bDrAfClGjgypnqHS1NlFbsIdsiqHKzA==')})
    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {
    Element.update('shelfListfalse_' + book_id, request.responseText)
    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

  function refreshGroupBox(group_id, book_id) {
    new Ajax.Updater('addGroupBooks' + book_id + '', '/group/add_book_box', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){refreshGroupBoxComplete(request, book_id);}, parameters:'id=' + group_id + '&book_id=' + book_id + '&refresh=true' + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('VxMNAFF0UNohXks9XXINufYrn1vOE7TTKDLvzJhep7NtNWTS6iqZsoZ1/1yqhwdmIHR4kwCeOrRzTKl9VR1VKQ==')})
  }

story with a startling twist, in which a husband seduces and impregnates a naïve servant; &quot;Cottagette,&quot; concerning the romance of a young artist and a man who\'s apparently too good to be true; &quot;Mr. Peebles\' Heart,&quot; a liberating tale of a fiftyish shopkeeper whose sister-in-law, a doctor, persuades him to take a solo trip to Europe, with revivifying results; &quot;The Yellow Wallpaper&quot;; and three other outstanding stories.\n\nThese charming tales are not only highly readable and full of humor and invention, but also offer ample food for thought about the social, economic, and personal relationship of men and women — and how they might be improved.\n\nThe yellow wallpaper \nThree Thanksgivings \nThe cottagette \nTurned \nMaking a change \nIf I were a man \nMr.       var newTip = new Tip($('bookCover_26530372'), "\n\n  <h2><a class=\"readable bookTitle\" href=\"https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/26530372-the-necklace-and-other-stories?from_choice=false&amp;from_home_module=false\">The Necklace and Other Stories: Maupassant for Modern Times<\/a><\/h2>\n\n      <div>\n        by <a class=\"authorName\" href=\"/author/show/18791.Guy_de_Maupassant?a=9&amp;origin=related_works\">Guy de Maupassant<\/a>\n      <\/div>\n\n          <div class=\"smallText uitext darkGreyText\">\n            <span class=\"minirating\"><span class=\"stars staticStars notranslate\"><span size=\"12x12\" class=\"staticStar p10\"><\/span><span size=\"12x12\" class=\"staticStar p10\"><\/span><span size=\"12x12\" class=\"staticStar p10\"><\/span><span size=\"12x12\" class=\"staticStar p6\"><\/span><span size=\"12x12\" class=\"staticStar p0\"><\/span><\/span> 3.96 avg rating &mdash; 15,832 ratings<\/span>            &mdash; published 1884\n          <\/div>\n\n    <div class=\"addBookTipDescription\">\n      \n<span id=\"freeTextContainer12373978754841948723\">A Parisian civil servant turned protégé of Flaubert, Guy de Maupassant is considered not only one of the greatest short story writers in all of French literature, but also a pioneer of psychological realism and modernism who helped define the form. C<\/span>\n  <span id=\"freeText12373978754841948723\" style=\"display:none\">A Parisian civil servant turned protégé of Flaubert, Guy de Maupassant is considered not only one of the greatest short story writers in all of French literature, but also a pioneer of psychological realism and modernism who helped define the form. Credited with influencing the likes of Chekhov, Maugham, Babel, and O. Henry, Maupassant had, at the time of his death at the age of forty-two, written six novels and some three hundred short stories. Yet in English, Maupassant has, curiously, remained unappreciated by modern readers due to outdated translations that render his prose in an archaic, literal style.\n\n\nIn this bold new translation, Sandra Smith—the celebrated translator of Irene Nemirovsky\'s Suite Francaise—brings us twenty-eight of Maupassant\'s essential stories and two novellas in lyrical yet accessible language that brings Maupassant into vibrant English. In addition to her sparkling translation, Smith also imposes a structure that captures the full range of Maupassant\'s work. Dividing the collection into three sections that reflect his predominant themes—nineteenth-century French society, the Franco-Prussian War of 1870–71, and the supernatural—Smith creates &quot;an arrangement suggesting a culture of relation, of structure, of completion&quot; (Richard Howard).\n\n\nIn &quot;Tales of French Life,&quot; we see Maupassant explore the broad swath of French society, not just examining the lives of the affluent as was customary for writers in his day. In the title story of the collection, &quot;The Necklace,&quot; Maupassant crafts a devastating portrait of misplaced ambition and ruin in the emerging middle class.\n\n\nThe stories in &quot;Tales of War&quot; emerge from Maupassant’s own experiences in the devastating Franco-Prussian War and create a portrait of that disastrous conflict that few modern readers have ever encountered. This section features Maupassant\'s most famous novella, &quot;Boule de Suif.&quot;\n\n\nThe last section, &quot;Tales of the Supernatural,&quot; delves into the occult and the bizarre. While certain critics may attribute some of these stories and morbid fascination as the product of the author\'s fevered mind and possible hallucinations induced by late-stage syphilis, they echo the gothic horror of Poe as well as anticipate the eerie fiction of H. P. Lovecraft.\n\n\nThe result takes readers from marriage, family, and the quotidian details of life to the disasters of war and nationalism, then to the gothic and beyond, allowing us to appreciate Maupassant in an idiom that matches our own times. The Necklace and Other Stories enables us to appreciate Maupassant as the progenitor of the modern short story and as a writer vastly ahead of his time.<\/span>\n  <a data-text-id=\"12373978754841948723\" href=\"#\" onclick=\"swapContent(\$(this));; return false;\">...more<\/a>\n\n    <\/div>\n\n      <div class=\'wtrButtonContainer For nearly a century, the work of O. Henry has delighted readers with its humor, irony and colorful, real-life settings. The writer\'s own life had more than a touch of color and irony. Born William Sidney Porter in Greensboro, North Carolina in 1862, he worked on a Texas ranch, then as a bank teller in Austin, then as a reporter for the Houston &quot;Post.&quot; Adversity struck, however, when he was indicted for embezzlement of bank funds. Porter fled to New Orleans, then to Honduras before he was tried, convicted and imprisoned for the crime in 1898. In prison he began writing stories of Central America and the American Southwest that soon became popular with magazine readers. After his release Porter moved to New York City, where he continued writing stories under the pen name O. Henry. \n\nThough his work earned him an avid readership, O. Henry died in poverty and oblivion scarcely eight years after his arrival in New York. But in the treasury of stories he left behind are such classics of the genre as &quot;The Gift of the Magi,&quot; &quot;The Last Leaf,&quot; &quot;The Ransom of Red Chief,&quot; &quot;The Voice of the City&quot; and &quot;The Cop and the Anthem&quot; — all included in this choice selection.       <div id="aboutAuthor" class=" clearFloats bigBox"><div class="h2Container gradientHeaderContainer"><h2 class="brownBackground"><a href="/author/show/1831.Jonathan_Swift">About Jonathan Swift</a></h2></div><div class="bigBoxBody"><div class="bigBoxContent containerWithHeaderContent">
          <div class='bookAuthorProfile'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__topContainer'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__photoContainer'>
<a href="/author/show/1831.Jonathan_Swift"><div class='bookAuthorProfile__photo' style='background-image: url(https://images.gr-assets.com/authors/1183238507p3/1831.jpg);'></div>
</a></div>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__widgetContainer'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__name'>
<a href="/author/show/1831.Jonathan_Swift">Jonathan Swift
</a></div>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__followerCount'>
1,353
followers
</div>

</div>
</div>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__about'>

<span id="freeTextContainer9676110573782518223">Jonathan Swift was an Anglo-Irish satirist, essayist, political pamphleteer (first for Whigs then for Tories), and poet, famous for works like <i>Gulliver's Travels</i>, <i>A Modest Proposal</i>, <i>A Journal to Stella</i>, <i>The Drapier's Letters</i>, <i>The Battle of the Books</i>, and <i>A Tale of a Tub</i>. Swift is probably the foremost prose satirist in the English language, and is less well known for his poetry. Swift published al</span>
  <span id="freeText9676110573782518223" style="display:none">Jonathan Swift was an Anglo-Irish satirist, essayist, political pamphleteer (first for Whigs then for Tories), and poet, famous for works like <i>Gulliver's Travels</i>, <i>A Modest Proposal</i>, <i>A Journal to Stella</i>, <i>The Drapier's Letters</i>, <i>The Battle of the Books</i>, and <i>A Tale of a Tub</i>. Swift is probably the foremost prose satirist in the English language, and is less well known for his poetry. Swift published all of his works under pseudonyms — such as Lemuel Gulliver, Isaac Bickerstaff, M.B. Drapier — or anonymously. He is also known for being a master of two styles of satire; the Horatian and Juvenalian styles.</span>
  <a data-text-id="9676110573782518223" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

</div>
</div>

This partial loads on almost every page view.  The associated React component makes
a call to SignInPromptController#get to determine if the user should see the sign in interstial.
This is determined by how many signed out pagehits the user has executed an how recently they have
last seen the insterstitial.  If the controller responds indicating the popup should appear, the
